B006MVJ2L4|5.0|Good music is good music, regardless of the genre.  We saw disco rise and fall (to much derision) not because disco was a bad genre of music, but because it became flooded with formulaic crap created to cash in on its popularity. But good disco was good music.  Today's pop music also has a lot of music assembled from parts known to be appealing, but without any substance or heart. But amid the product generated to fill up the airwaves there exists quality music that will someday be listed in a catalog called the Golden Age of Pop - music created by Lady Gaga, Pink, Usher, and more.  In this company enters Adam Lambert's Trespassing.<br /><br />The album is unexpectedly split into two parts, with all of the upbeat, dance tunes grouped together at the front. They're bright and happy, with witty lyrics, a solid dance beat, and polished, interesting arrangements.  Rather than being dispersed thru the album and interspersed with the more reflective ballads, as is more common, the grouping allows the energy to build and create a real club feel.  They're perfect for parties or exercising, but are also interesting enough to just sit and listen to.  The second half of the album is a very textured collection of ballads.  Since Lambert wrote on 12 of the 15 tracks on the deluxe version of the album, these songs are personal to him and capture an emotional content that is often rare in pop music.<br /><br />Beyond the songs, the arrangements, and the production there is \\"that voice.\\" People refer to Lambert's voice in quotations because it is extraordinary.  He has a multi-octave range that is as fully developed and controlled in the lower register and when he sings softly, as it is in the big, belted high notes that are almost de rigueur in today's hit songs.  Lambert can transition from sweet, soft, tender notes, through a rock and roll growl, to a full-throated, ringing tone, and up into a pitch-pure falsetto with an ease that is astonishing.  More importantly, he knows exactly when to employ each technique, with exquisite timing and musicianship for maximum effect, without the listener ever feeling that it's calculated or self-conscious. One song is not enough to appreciate the breadth of Lambert's vocal gifts and talent. You need to listen to them all!

0439023513|5.0|WARNING SPOILERS:<br />I feel this book tells a tragic tale of a fallen heroine who realizes that there is a high price for freedom in love and war. Suzanne Collins sends this message:  Are we ever truly free or at peace, since truly there will always be a ruling figure or government?  There are so many dark social issues that the first book alluded to that I found book 3 to be appropriately unsettling: the outcomes of war, PTSD and other mental disorders linked to traumatic events, socialism, commercialism, propaganda through television, idolization of celebrities, etc.  This trilogy reminded me in some ways of the premise for \\"American Psycho\\" starring Christian Bale.  It's a different story but also offers a satirical interpretation of our materialist impulsive driven society. The dark undertones were present throughout the series in all the characters, even Peeta.  A growing distance between Gale and Katniss was imminent.  Gale explains to Katniss in book 3 that he wished he took Peeta's place to care for Katniss but he knew he had a responsibility to care for her family during her absence.  In Book 2, he announced his purpose to start a rebellion while Katniss continued to be a pawn for the Capitol in the Quarter Quells. Peeta on the other hand, having all good human characteristics, eventually is broken down through the atrocities of war and never recovers. He symbolizes justice and truth and when he is broken down, Katniss weakens and struggles with her own truths.  Social morale tests these characters' convictions.  History has shown how social norms change and adapt to culture, moral issues change with social changes and continues to shape the future of society (i.e. Civil Rights Moevement, slavery abolition, abortion, gay rights, etc.) Peeta and Katniss' facade became their reality once they realized they had to adapt and move on with life. She was struggling to fight the system all along, both the Capitol and the Rebels (both dystopian societies), but fighting it alone in the end once the war was over.  This reminds me of what happened during 9/11.  People completely forgot about the victims, it was glorified on tv and still is, but the victims still suffer and many are long forgotten.  It's like most wars really (especially Vietnam).  The 3rd book touched a lot of social issues that reflect the problems with today's societies.<br />This quote was quite poignant to me in Book 3:<br /><br />'\\"Are we preparing for another war, Plutarch?'\\" I ask.<br />'\\"Oh, not now. Now we're in that sweet period where everyone agrees that our recent horrors should never be repeated,\\" he says.  \\"But collective thinking is usually short-lived.  We're fickle, stupid beings with poor memories and a great gift for self-destruction.  Although who knows?  Maybe this will be it, Katniss.\\"\\"<br /><br />After this quote, Plutarch, the game keeper, changes the subject abruptly to continue planning a singing program for Katniss as if that has more significance.<br /><br />There are other wonderful quotes in this book that show a deeper underlying message.  To those who only enjoyed Book 1 and 2, do you think that you may be as guilty as the Capitol citizens for being entertained by children fighting to the death in the Hunger Games?  Are we not doing that today in our society too by buying into media portrayals of celebrities, current events and tragedies? I think Collins wants to show that the citizens are as much as fault, both those in support and those opposing government, as those in power in perpetuating social injustices and norms; that it comes at a high moral price. No matter how strong, we are all vulnerable and fallible when faced with death and trauma.  Just something to think about...

1451627289|5.0|Who says you can't write a book about time travel and not have it be science fiction? Not Stephen King!<br /><br />Actually, King has dabbled in time travel before, as it played a part in his Dark Tower series. But in 11/22/63, where time travel is front and center, it's the fiction, not the science, that impresses.<br /><br />Simply put, 11/22/63 is a book full of nostalgia. Longing for a simpler time fairly drips from every page. It's a love letter to an era before computers, cell phones, the Internet, terrorism, globalism, and best-selling Stephen King novels. Of course, people who lived during those decades had their own problems. The Cold War. The Cuban Missile Crisis. Out-of-control cigarette smoking. Racism. And, oh yes, the assassination of a president.<br /><br />Readers who weren't alive during the 50s and 60s might not relate quite as strongly to the subject matter as those who lived through the events King so masterfully describes.  But even younger readers (and by \\"younger\\" I mean anyone under 50) will finish with no doubt about how Mr. King feels about those decades. And when you fall as deeply in love with mid-20th-century culture as the author and his main character, chances are you'll end up wishing you had access to a portal through time as well.<br /><br />King does a great job focusing on the story and for the most part letting the various paradoxes traditionally associated with time travel in literature alone. In this book, time travel is just assumed to be possible. Never mind how it works or why. When one character asks, \\"What if you go back and kill your grandfather?\\" Another answers, quite correctly, it seems to me, \\"Why the %&$# would you want to do a thing like that?\\" End of discussion. No endless angst over the space-time continuum. It's refreshing.<br /><br />As ever, King's prose is perhaps a tad shy of literary genius, but that's really not what he's shooting for. In this book, he's hoping to entertain, to inform, and to transport readers 50 years into the past to see \\"what might have happened if.\\" And at that, there is none better than Stephen King. Maybe he's a genius after all.<br /><br />This strong, character-driven book contains less language and sexuality than some of King's others, and despite the rather dour undercurrent of the looming assassination, the book manages not to be overly dark or hopeless. If you enjoy Stephen King books, you'll enjoy this one too. He's just too good a writer not to like his work. And if you've never read a Stephen King book before, this might be a good introduction. Highly recommended--but beware; it's long, and even when you get to the final page, you might not want it to end.ers 50 years into the past to see \\"what might have happened if.\\" And at that, there is none better than Stephen King. Maybe he's a genius after all.<br /><br />This strong, character-driven book contains less language and sexuality than some of King's others, and despite the rather dour undercurrent of the looming assassination, the book manages not to be overly dark or hopeless. Highly recommended--but beware; it's long, and even when you get to the final page, you might not want it to end.

The Play version mentions that it uses Openfeint, though it is completely optional and up to the user whether or not to enable it. Openfeint is NOT a horrendous thing that others make it out to be. It's a leader board system so people can post scores competitively against other players. It's NOT a data miner (the favorite phrase that the paranoid ignorance of Amazon loves to throw around). It accesses your scores so it can post them to be compared with others. That's it! It's simply a multilayer feature for those that wish to use it. If you don't feel comfortable using it, you're not forced to. I've noticed most Amazon users are clueless as to what Openfeint actually is.<br />Regardless, this is a game that shouldn't be missed!that shouldn't be missed!

I am no stranger to unsympathetic heroines (\\"The Custom of the Country\\" my favorite of Edith Wharton's books) and I know Ana Steele was initially based on Bella Swan, who is apparently spineless, but if James was going to release the story as a stand-alone, then she should have re-thought and re-written her heroine.  Ana spends a great deal of her time wondering why Christian wants her, and by the time I finished the third book, I found myself asking that question, too.  He says she challenges him.  How exactly does she do that?  Every time he growls at her, glowers at her (there was a lot of growling and glowering) she caves in to his demands.  She even let him beat her because he wanted to, not because she wanted it.  Her feelings, at best, were somewhat ambivalent about all the spanking, yet she let him do it.  Doesn't sound like much of a challenge to me.<br /><br />Secondly, the character of Christian I found a bit more interesting but I was left unsatisfied by his development.  I guess it's supposed to be a female fantasy to fix a broken man, but does James really intend us to believe that Ana accomplished in less than two months what 20+ years of intensive therapy could not?  So all this man really needed was the love of a good woman to get him to give up almost 15 years of BDSM?  I call shenanigans. And then, after three books full of sturm un drang, the big reveal of the root of his issues was anticlimactic.  Anyone with an IQ over 75 could see the it coming a mile away.  His predilections would have made more sense if Elliot and Mia had been Grace and Carrick's natural children, with Christian being the only one adopted.  That way, when he says the thing about not being perfect in a family full of perfect people, it would have had more weight behind it.<br /><br />I think the books would have been far more interesting had the author:<br /><br />1.  Resisted the chance to make more money and simply condensed the books into one.  There was no point in making a trilogy of this lightweight story.  A denser, more nuanced story would have been more satisfying to the reader.<br /><br />2.  Explored the BDSM aspect a bit more without all of the unnecessary sex scenes.  Since she is apparently into plagiarism, she should have copied A. N. Roqeulaure's (Ann Rice, for the uninitiated) \\"Sleeping Beauty\\" trilogy.  The story would have been more compelling had Ana actually allowed herself to explore Christian's lifestyle a bit.  There would have been a chance for her to understand why he likes to bind, gag and spank pretty brunettes without all of the tedious questioning about Leila, Mrs. Robinson and all the other subs.  This would have given some density to the stories and allowed the reader to fully understand why she leaves him.  As it is, I thought she was pretty damned stupid for storming out the way she did.  After all, what did she expect when she let him beat her with a belt?  I know she said that she had never been physically disciplined by her parents, but even so, she couldn't have expected it to tickle.<br /><br />3.  Allowed Ana and Christian more time during the break up.  That way, Ana could date a few other people, experience sex with someone other than a sadistic control freak, and then decide if she really wants to spend the rest of her life with a sore ass.  It would also have allowed Christian to go back to his lifestyle and perhaps slowly discover that it wasn't as fulfilling as being with Ana.  But the way James paced things, the reconciliation seemed so unbelievable.  After only five days without this woman we're supposed to believe that Christian is so overwrought that he decides to abandon his sexual behaviors on a whim?<br /><br />A great many of the problems with this book could have been overcome with something as simple as a night school course in creative writing.  It seems like there was kernel of a good idea in this book but the author completely squandered her chance to expand on it.

B003L16FAE|1.0|Predator is a classic sci-fi action film, and fans have been waiting for a proper release of this film on Blu-ray. When Fox announced the Ultimate Hunter Edition, we all rejoiced. That is until we seen the quality of the movie on the disc.<br /><br />The movie has undergone a heavy scrubbing with the use of Digital Noise Reduction (DNR). For whatever reason, studios are using the more and more on catalog titles released on Blu-ray. Sadly, because most people don't understand what DNR is and what it does to the image.<br /><br />Digital Noise Reduction is an automated process done to the image to reduce noise and artifacts on the video. DNR can be used to a positive effect (see the recent remaster of the Alien films for example), but studios are overdoing it. If you look at the image on this edition of the Blu-ray, you'll see that the texture of people's skin has been made almost as smooth as a Barbie doll. What the DNR has done is not only remove the noise from the video, but also all the fine detail that Blu-ray is supposed to provide. You're actually getting less detail than the standard DVD because of all the missing video information that has been scrubbed away.<br /><br />If you'd like to see other movies that have been the victim of heavy DNR, check out the Blu-rays of American Psycho, Tremors, Walking Tall, and Mr. & Mrs. Smith.

0151013837|4.0|This is a submission for an English Composition course.<br /><br /> `Life of Pi' is a riveting fictional novel about Piscine Patel, a young Indian boy whose family decides to sell their family zoo animals to move to Canada. The story is told from the perspective of Piscine or Pi as well as the journalist interviewing Pi in his later life. The carrier ship that Pi, his family, and their animals were travelling upon sinks in the middle of the ocean. There is much foreboding foreshadowing that takes place at the beginning of the novel that lets the reader know such a tragic event is going to take place.<br /> Although a tale of survival, the themes of storytelling and belief in God are present. From the beginning, when observing the zoo animals in India, Pi reflects \\"I don't mean to defend zoos. ...I know zoos are no longer in people's good graces. Religion faces the same problem. Certain illusions about freedom plague them both\\" (Martel, 14). As a young boy in India, Pi takes a great interest in spirituality and religion; he becomes a practitioner of Christianity, Hinduism, and Muslim. \\"A germ of religious exaltation, no bigger than a mustard seed, was sown in me and left to germinate. It has never stopped growing since that day\\" admits Pi (Martel, 25). Once thrown into the situation of having to survive, Pi attempts to find the presence of God and continues to pray, \\"I have survived so far, miraculously. Now I will turn miracle into routine...Yes, so long as God is with me, I will not die. Amen.\\" (Martel, 250). The reader is presented with the notion that it's not so much the particular religion one chooses but the belief they have in God that is of importance. Pi fully relates this when stating \\"If you stumble at mere believability, what are you living for? Isn't love hard to believe?...ask any lover. Life is hard to believe, ask any scientist. God is hard to believe, ask any believer.\\" (Martel, 292).<br /> The majority of the novel describes the measures Pi goes through in his determination of survival. His lifeboat initially contains himself, an orangutan, a zebra, a hyena, and a Bengal tiger named Mr. Richard Parker. Quickly the laws of nature reign when the elements at sea begin to affect every living thing on this small boat. Eventually there is just Pi and Richard Parker attempting to coexist between the boat and a small lifejacket raft Pi attached to the boat. They survive on rain water, fish, and the occasional sea turtle. Pi is in constant fear of Mr. Richard Parker, a predator never far but must be dominated if Pi wishes to live.<br />The art and act of storytelling is perfected by the author Yann Martel in how the character Pi thoroughly describes these hardships. The entire story is enrapturing but within chapter 99, when Pi is questioned by Mr. Okamoto, a man sent to investigate how and why the carrier ship sank, is when the most compelling part of the novel takes place. When Mr. Okamoto does not believe Pi's story of survival that includes the animals, especially Richard Parker, Pi tells the same story where each animal is replaced by a living person from the carrier ship (Pi's mother, sailors, the cook). The horrors of death and cannibalism become much more gruesome and unsettling with this telling and Pi then asks \\"'So tell me, since it makes no factual difference to you and you can't prove the question either way, which story do you prefer? Which is the better story, the story with animals or the story without animals?'...Mr. Okamoto: Yes. The story with animals is the better story. Pi Patel: `Thank you. And so it goes with God.'\\" (Martel, 317). It makes no factual difference whether God does or does not exist; the `better story' filled with light and hope then is to believe that God does exist.<br /><br />Martel, Yann. Life of Pi. New York: Harcourt, Inc., 2001.

I called this review \\"the fine line between experimentation and self-indulgence\\" because I'm not sure where this falls; but I suspect it is more towards the latter. So I give it a 3-star rating: it is beautifully put together, and it is certainly unique; but given the pedigrees of the artists involved, it is too close to pretentious noodling. But maybe it's not. B005DI994W|5.0|The Four Feathers remains one of the great classics of British cinema.  An Alexander Korda production (and directed by his brother, Zoltan Korda) that shows what they mean by the expression, \\"They don't make them like this anymore.\\"<br /><br />Filmed largely on location in the Sudanese desert where the story took place, the story follows ex-soldier Harry Faversham who seeks to make up for his believed cowardice by rescuing the three men who gave him white feathers of cowardice.  John Clements gives a lowkey but powerful performance as Haversham.  Other excellent members of the cast are June Deprez (who would next do The Thief of Bagdad), Ralph Richardson, and C. Aubrey Smith.<br /><br />All of the battle scenes with hundreds of extras are incredible.  No computer-generated effects here.<br /><br />The video is very good, with an occasional line and dropout.  The color is rich, though there are occasionally moments that colors outline seems slightly off, looking the way 3D might be if you didn't put on the glasses.<br /><br />Extras include a video introduction by David Korda, the son of the director.  There is also a nice short of the Denham Studios from 1939, in which you can see a few scenes from the movie being filmed. The highlight of the extras is a non-stop, very informative commentary by film historian, Charles Drazin.  If only all commentaries were this well-researched.  Enclosed in the case is a fold-out essay by film critic Michael Sragow<br /><br />Highly recommended Criterion release!

B007I2BZIE|4.0|I've listened to this CD now three times, and my excitement and joy that I've felt since the first listen haven't been abated.  Clockwork Angels truly is a multi-layered conceptual work of art.  Geddy's bass playing is very much in the forefront and his virtuosity shines through without coming across as ostentatious and still blends well with the other instrumentation in the mix.  Harmonies and vocal layering are the same: peppered throughout the tracks to accent various different lyrics.  Alex's guitar work is as brilliant as ever, varying from simple to complex, haunting to background, acoustic to distorted with wonderful solos throughout.  Neil's drumming and lyrics are as concise and poignant as always, and the overall concept of the album is just, well, cool.  I'm in love with all things Steam Punk, so I'm biased.  The only thing that prevents me from issuing a 5 star rating is that despite the narrative approach of the lyrics, there are a few instances where it seems that it is not the character talking to us, but Neil himself.  While there is always a part of the author in the character, I think that dedication to the concept would keep the wall between author and reader a little less transparent.  For the majority of this CD, Geddy's high notes are flawless, but as Geddy is getting older, he is losing some control of the upper register of his voice.  It sucks, but it happens.  In the Seven Cities of Gold, the quality of his singing suffers slightly due to the sustained higher range of the vocals. Finally, for some reason, there is no information on any of the tracks.  Everything is listed as Unknown Album, Track 1.  It's a small point and may be nitpicking, but in the digital age, it seems like a gross oversight. What Will and Julie uncover cannot only change the course of their lives but everyone that walks the planet as well.<br /><br />David Baldacci knows how to take the facts, write them into a work of genius that will keep the reader unable to put the book down.  The twists and turns in THE INNOCENT are a masterful manipulation of fiction that comes so close to fact it if frightening.

If you don't know why you would shoot in any mode but AUTO, or only have a vague sense of the things you can do by creatively controlling aperture and shutter speed then you will benefit from this book, hands down!<br /><br />Here's the bottom line: your camera doesn't know Jack about taking awesome pictures, in only knows how to get a technically correct exposure. But a technically correct exposure is a far cry from a creatively correct one. There are many possible ways to correctly expose an image, and knowing and understanding those methods will make a night and day difference in your photography.<br /><br />The book itself is an easy read and not too long. Peterson doesn't dwell for too long on any one topic - just gives you the info you need and moves on while remaining entertaining throughout. I find myself referring to various sections often as I've been seeking to improve my photography.<br /><br />Do yourself a favor and buy this book.
